In the 1st Wilder and Fury fight, you will see if you watch this video at 1:10 - https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=3ZRDVA-u3Io it will help you to see what I mean, that no ref would start a count as soon as the fighter hits the canvas the majority of timekeepers/ref's would start their count half of a second or a second after the fighter hits the canvas no one is 100% precise while counting. If you do go along with the time in the bottom right corner from 1:10 of the video I provided above you would see that Fury is getting up on the count of 9 to 9.5 seconds and fully standing at 10. The ref starts his count at 2:22 just after Fury goes down and at 2:19 the ref is at the count of 4 and by the time Fury gets to his feet and almost standing straight is at 2:13 the count is at 9 and at 2:12 which would have been the count of 10 the ref asks Fury can he continue which Fury replies yes most top ref's would let the fighter continue if he is getting up on 9.5 anyway!   In the 3rd fight between Wilder and Fury, Wilder didn't go back to the neutral corner! That's why the ref briefly stopped the count. 4.) KNOCKDOWN, KNOCKOUT AND COUNT4.4. When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knock-downs. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knock-down, the timekeeper's count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers. Nonetheless, the ref is at the count of 7 when he should have been at 8 but that was down to Wilder not returning to a neutral corner quicker so Fury beat the count fairly.

Billy Bird (1 January 1899 â€“ 6 February 1951) was a British professional boxer who was active from 1920 to 1948 and boxed in the welterweight division. He fought a recorded 356 times in his 28 year-career and was regarded as one of the most active boxers of his time. He holds the record of the most knockouts in any fighting career. Bird has 356 recorded fights, but has been rumored to have even more fights during his career which were not professional matches. Bird currently holds the knockout world record at 138. Once the ref picks up the count, that's the official count, the referee can pace it at his own discretion. It's not a 10 second count, it rarely ever comes to 10 seconds precisely. If the opponent steps out of the corner and the ref has to tell him to go back, the ref is fully within his right to continue where he left off, lots of refs do it as it acts as a deterrent / punishment to the standing fighter for messing around. That being said, Fury's eyes were on the ref the entire time, he was just making use of the extra time, he still would have been up on his feet had the count been any faster.

This is the rules from the WBF - Joe I love ya but your wrong the ref picks up the count from where he left off if interrupted by the standing fighter. please correct your corruption comment.   4.4.         When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted.

Hay guys. Just to clear up the count rules,if a Fighter doesn't go to a neutral corner the referee rule is to stop the count,make fighter go to corner,then continue the count,that's the rules,stop the count then continue count once other fighter goes to the corner,not continue count during this time. Watch the replays in slow motion. The referee picked the count up from the time keeper on 4 correctly too. So as Tyson get up the count is on 5,but the count isn't meant to be 10 seconds because it's based on the recovery rules not exact count of seconds. The recovery rules state that the referee as counting is also looking to see the eyes and legs are back and they can take instructions,as in gloves up come towards me.. the ref seen Tyson's eyes and legs were ok on 8 then he told Tyson gloves up and move. It was not a long count at all because the rules again state the count is at the referee's discretion as they must give the fighter time to recover,it's a golden rule,and has never been an exact 10 seconds count ever. It's a recovery count at the referee's discretion because the show must go on as such,unless the fighter is out on his feet. It has always been this way.. every count in boxing is longer than 10 seconds,it's a 10 count not a 10 second count.. The ref outranks everyone, including the timekeeper. The ref guides the timekeeper not the other way round. If the standing fighter leaves the neutral corner the ref is to suspend the count and pick up from where he left off. That's the penalty for not going to the neutral corner quickly and staying there. That's how it works in boxing, which definitely isn't the sport people seem to think it is. It's not about who can punch harder, it's not a race to a 10 second knockdown. It's the art of punching to the body and head while dodging punches from your opponent. The ref is supposed to use his discretion to see if a fighter can continue and still defend themselves intelligently. You have up to 12 rounds to beat your opponent to the point they can't continue to box, or to land more clean punches on the legal target than they do on you.

There is a slight mistake in the rules interpretation. When a person is knocked down, the time keeper starts the count while the ref sends the other fighter to a neutral corner. That part was correct. If the standing fighter strays from the neutral corner (like Wilder did) the ref will  pause the count as a punishment/deterrent for wandering from the neutral corner.  This also assumes Fury wouldn't have otherwise gotten up. He looked aware and wanted to take his time.   The lesson: stay in the neutral corner.

*** Joe Rogan is incorrect, as a former boxer I have seen this happen many times. First off, the referee may halt the count at any time to make sure the fighter scoring the knockdown went to a neutral corner. "Dempsey/Tunney long count"..  Number 2, the count is not 10 seconds, its the ref's count of 10. Most 10 counts in boxing through out the last 100 years are around 14-15 seconds, boxing referee's do count at slightly different speeds. But it's never been a clocked 10 second count. Ali/Liston 2, Tyson/Douglas all had funny counts, the only thing that the fallen fighter can do is listen to count from the 3rd man in the ring. Many fighters may have gotten up faster had the count be faster. Seasoned pro fighters are trained to take a knee and rise at 8 1/2 so your on your feet at 9. Most Refs today take additional time closely examining the fighter upon rising post 10 count., They ask questions, name? What month is it? Do u want to continue? They also ask for lateral steps to either the right of left to assess gait to look for instability which could mean a concussion. Nothing is more important than making sure a fighter has all his facilities when your turn loose against a trained killer who is looking to put him away. I like the idea that they don't rush the counts. Marciano/Walcott 2 had a situation where Walcott got dropped in the first round by a weak punch and rose at 9 1/2 being perfectly fine and got counted out. The fans where furious, similar to Tyson/Carl Williams. Refs should give a fighter every chance to continue after a knockdown early in a fight, particularly when they can defend themselves.

1927 Dempsey vs Tunney  A new rule instituted at the time of the fight mandated that when a fighter knocked down an opponent, he must immediately go to a neutral corner. Dempsey, however, refused to immediately move to the neutral corner when instructed by the referee. The referee had to escort Dempsey to the neutral corner, which bought Tunney at least an extra five seconds to recover. Even though the official timekeeper clocked 14 seconds Tunney was down, Tunney got up at the referee's count of 9.

KNOCKDOWN, KNOCKOUT AND COUNT  4.1.Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â A boxer shall be considered â€œdownâ€ when, as a result of a legal blow as ruled by the referee, who is the only person authorized to determine this, any part of their body other than their feet are on the canvas, or if they are hanging helplessly over the ropes, or if they are still rising from the down position and not standing upright.  4.2.Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â The Mandatory Eight (8) Count after knockdowns will be standard procedure in all World Boxing Federation championship contests. There is no Standing Eight (8) Count; there is no Three (3) Knockdown Rule. If in the refereesâ€™ judgement the knockdowns have been indecisive and clearly with no injurious effect upon the boxer, the contest may be continued but with good sense and judgement and the boxersâ€™ welfare always paramount.  4.3.Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall audibly announce the count as he motions with his right arm downward indicating the end of each sound of the count. If the boxer taking the count is still down when the referee calls the count of ten (10), the referee shall wave both arms indicating that the boxer has been knocked out.  4.4.Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knockdown, the timekeepers count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers.  4.5.Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â A boxer shall receive a twenty (20) second count if the boxer is knocked out of the ring and onto the floor. The boxer is to be unassisted by spectators or his/her seconds. If assisted by anyone, the boxer may lose points or be disqualified with such a decision being within the sole discretion of the referee.  4.6.Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â A boxer who has been knocked down cannot be saved by the bell in any round. If a boxer is down and the round has terminated, the referee shall continue the count until the boxer rises or is counted out. If a boxer fails to rise before the count of ten (10), he shall be declared the loser by knockout in the round just concluded.

10 Count not a 10 Second count...plus The ref can not continue to count until the opposing fighter Is in a neutral corner  it's always been like this and will not change..
NAC467.740. Procedure for counting; knockdown; knockout; technical draw  Â  1. When an unarmed combatant is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to retire to the farthest neutral corner of the ring, by pointing to the corner, and shall immediately begin the count over the unarmed combatant who is down. The referee shall audibly announce the passing of the seconds, accompanying the count with motions of his or her arm, with the downward motion indicating the end of each second.  Â Â Â Â  2. The timekeeper, by effective signaling, shall give the referee the correct 1-second interval for the count. The refereeâ€™s count is the official count. Once the referee picks up the count from the timekeeper, the timekeeper shall cease counting. No unarmed combatant who is knocked down may be allowed to resume competing until the referee has finished counting to eight. The unarmed combatant may take the count either on the floor or standing.  Â Â Â Â  3. If the opponent fails to stay in the farthest corner, the referee shall cease counting until the opponent has returned to his or her corner and shall then go on with the count from the point at which it was interrupted. If the unarmed combatant who is down arises before the count of 10, the referee may step between the unarmed combatants long enough to assure himself or herself that the unarmed combatant who has just arisen is in condition to continue. If so assured, the referee shall, without loss of time, order both unarmed combatants to go on with the contest or exhibition. During the intervention by the referee, the striking of a blow by either unarmed combatant may be ruled a foul.  Â Â Â Â  4. When an unarmed combatant is knocked out, the referee shall perform a full 10-second count unless, in the judgment of the referee, the safety of the unarmed combatant would be jeopardized by such a count. If the unarmed combatant who is knocked down is still down when the referee calls the count of 10, the referee shall wave both arms to indicate that he or she has been knocked out.  Â Â Â Â  5. If both unarmed combatants go down at the same time, the count must be continued as long as one is still down. If both unarmed combatants remain down until the count of 10, the contest or exhibition must be stopped and the decision is a technical draw.  Â Â Â Â  6. If an unarmed combatant is down and the referee is in the course of counting at the end of a period of unarmed combat, the bell indicating the end of the period of unarmed combat must not be sounded, but the bell must be sounded as soon as the downed unarmed combatant regains his or her feet.  Â Â Â Â  7. When an unarmed combatant has been knocked down before the normal termination of a period of unarmed combat and the period of unarmed combat terminates before he or she has arisen from the floor of the ring, the refereeâ€™s count must be continued. If the unarmed combatant who is down fails to arise before the count of 10, he or she is considered to have lost the contest or exhibition by a knockout in the round containing the period of unarmed combat that was just concluded.  Â Â Â Â  8. If a legal blow struck in the final seconds of a period of unarmed combat causes an unarmed combatant to go down after the bell has sounded, that knockdown must be regarded as having occurred during the period of unarmed combat just ended and the appropriate count must continue.  Â Â Â Â  9. The provisions of this section do not apply to a contest or exhibition of mixed martial arts.  (Hope joe rogan can see this)

Joe is incorrect.  The ref picks up the count from the time keeper who starts counting immediately after the ref signals a KD.  Then after sending the other fighter to the neutral corner, the ref then picks up the count from the time keeper.  If for whatever reason that fighter doesn't stay in the neutral corner, or in Wilders case goes to the wrong corner, the count stops until the ref gets him into the neutral corner.  The ref was correct albeit slow with his counting, but he was the same with Wilder.

Their takes on the long count is so wrong. Especially in regards to whether or not the refs should be doing the count. The 10 count is not a 10 second count. The whole point of the 10 count is for the referee to evaluate the health and awareness of the boxer to see if he is fit to continue fighting. 10 is just a standard so the boxer is aware of how long they have to recover and show they are fit to continue. In high profile fights, the count is usually longer than lower profile fights given the importance. Just like in mma. A referee will give more time/chances for a fighter to show they are fit to continue

According to the rules of the New York and New Jersey boxing commissions, a knockout is not necessarily 10 seconds, but rather a count of 10.  "A 10-count is not done by a stopwatch," said Larry Hazzard, a former referee and now chairman of the New Jersey State Athletic Control Board. "That's splitting hairs. The question is, did the fighter beat the count and can he continue?"

Rules are in short, if the ref has to stop and rediirect the opponenet to neutral corner, he has to pick up the count where he was interrupted. The WBF rules (Rule 4.4) clearly state counting rules as follows: "Â Â When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knockdown, the timekeepers count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers."

Sorry Joe, you're wrong about the count. This has been analyzed over and over by people who know more about boxing than you, and the count was legit. It's supposed to be a 10-count, made by the ref, at the ref's pace, this is specifically clarified in the rules of boxing. It is NOT specifically supposed to be 10-seconds exactly. Also, if he has to stop counting because the other corner does something wrong (such as not going to a neutral corner, like WIlder did in this case), the count is SUPPOSED TO STOP while the ref addresses it. This is INTENTIONAL. It is meant to be a penalty to the corner for not doing what they were supposed to do. Also, Tyson is CLEARLY watching the ref, waiting for the count to get up. If the ref had counted faster, Tyson would have gotten up faster, this is obvious when you watch the replays.

When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knockdown, the timekeepers count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers.

*WBF* "When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knockdown, the timekeepers count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers."

For those who wonder how the ref counts; when a knockdown occurs the fighter must go to a neutral corner. At that point the timekeeper will begin the count, and the ref will pick up that count, which is why youâ€™ll often hear them start counting at 3 or 4. According to World Boxing Federation: When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knockdown, the timekeepers count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers.

06:30 Joe is incorrect, when a fighter is knocked down a guy at ringside will immediately start the count, the ref will direct the standing fighter to the farthest neutral corner, the ref will then pick up the count from the ringside dude and continue it on, at that point the ringside guy is done with any counting. If the fighter in the neutral corner leaves that corner DURING the count the ref can stop the count to tell that fighter to get his ass back in the neutral corner, he will then pick up his own count from when he stopped it.  That is why you see fighter get to that neutral corner with a quickness and stay there, if a fighter leaves i during the count they are stupid.

Boxing matches have an official timekeeper who starts counting seconds as soon as a boxer is on the ground. At the same time, the referee pauses the fight, sends the other standing boxer to a neutral corner, and then picks up the count from the timekeeper. This is why you usually see the ref start counting at 3 or 4, and not 1.  Once the ref picks up the count, itâ€™s up to them to finish at their pace. For example, if the standing boxer doesnâ€™t stay in their corner then the ref will pause the count to give more time to the fallen boxer as a deterrence.  Notice that itâ€™s called the 10-count rule and not the 10-second rule. The count doesnâ€™t have to be exactly accurate, and it almost never is.  When the count is excessively long, itâ€™s called a slow count or a long count, but itâ€™s not illegal because itâ€™s up to the ref, even if it causes a lot of controversy.

Boxing rules require the timekeeper, after a knockdown, to immediately begin calling off the seconds, indicating the count with a motion of his arm. After ordering the other boxer to a neutral corner, the referee is to pick up the count from the timekeeper, indicating it with a motion of his arm.  "Once the ref picks up the count, that's the official count," referee Mills Lane, who regularly works fights in Nevada, told the Associated Press.

He's wrong. The ref that officiated the first fight, explains why they count the way that they do. You can find that video on YouTube. They count them out if they are out, if they are aware, if they look them in the face, they are already reading the fighters body language. The reason he isn't counting super quick, Tyson is looking at him, he's not limp, so he's going to get up once he hits 7 whether that's a moment slow or quick. More importantly than the count is that body language, and then whether they have strength when they check that, then they make them turn and take a step, if they can't do any of that, if they are slurry, the fight is over. The count really isn't what is important, everything else tells them if the fight should continue. They ask themselves if the fighter can defend themselves intelligently. Then they make a decision. The ref is right, we have hindsight, and Fury went on to win. Clearly he could still fight. He did the same thing for Wilder. The rules on the count are not quite as Joe describes. The ringside counter starts the count the moment the fighter hits the floor. The ref then syncs with him and picks up the count. From that moment on, it's up to the ref how quickly he counts, depending on many factors. Contrary to popular belief, it's never meant necessarily to be a ten second count, but simply a ten count.

Big John Mccarthy explained it himself. The rules state: â€œKeep an eye on the standing boxer and make sure he stays in neutral corner. If boxer comes out, stop the count, put boxer back into neutral corner and then resume counting.â€ Joe is right in that when the knockdown first happens the ref picks up the count from the ringside counter if needed. But if the count is interrupted part way through by a fighter leaving the neutral corner then the ref stops the count, tells him to return, then picks up where he left off. This rule is to keep fighters in the neutral corner or they risk giving their opponent more time to recover. Its the simplist and best way to ensure a fighter conforms to the rules, if they don't they are effectively punished by a long count for their opponent.

Boxing matches have an official timekeeper who starts counting seconds as soon as a boxer is on the ground. At the same time, the referee pauses the fight, sends the other standing boxer to a neutral corner, and then picks up the count from the timekeeper. This is why you usually see the ref start counting at 3 or 4, and not 1.  Once the ref picks up the count, itâ€™s up to them to finish at their pace. For example, if the standing boxer doesnâ€™t stay in their corner then the ref will pause the count to give more time to the fallen boxer as a deterrence.  Notice that itâ€™s called the 10-count rule and not the 10-second rule. The count doesnâ€™t have to be exactly accurate, and it almost never is.  When the count is excessively long, itâ€™s called a slow count or a long count, but itâ€™s not illegal because itâ€™s up to the ref, even if it causes a lot of controversy.

Yeah, Joe's wrong about the referee/ringside counter counting for the knockdowns. Because think about it, if the standing fighter takes so long to get to a neutral corner that the ringside counter gets to 10 before the referee can pick up the count, is the referee supposed to wave the fight over? Is the downed fighter supposed to be watching the timekeeper?  Basically, the referee gets to determine what the count is in a given situation. Plenty of refs will count even if the standing fighter isn't in the neutral corner. It only really becomes an issue if the referee's count is egregiously long, or comically fast, or inconsistent between the two fighters.

Stay in your neutral corner and the count won't stop.   The Rule:Stay The time keeper starts the count when the fighter goes down. The ref picks up the count from the time keeper. Once the ref picks up the count that becomes the official count from that point forward.  If he has to stop to tell the other fighter to go to the neutral corner, he stops his official count and starts it back up. The time keeper is out of the equation at the point.

Joe Rogan you are wrong on the 10 second count situation, boxing rules are that it is a 10 count not a 10 second count,  so the discretion is up to the referee also there is an official outside the ring who begins the count as soon as the opponent is knocked down,  And the referee takes up the officials count and then it becomes the referees count, If an opponent leaves the neutral corner the referee orders the opponent back into the neutral corner, the ref then continues the count, what people who are ignorant of the rules of boxing, They believe the count is 10 seconds to get the boxer out of there, the 10 count is an opportunity to give the fighter a chance to recover safely and continue the fight if possible not for some  Referee to count a hundred miles an hour to end the fight, the ignorance of the rules of boxing by some people is staggering, never mind joe Your still a legend and you get 1% of stuff wrong this being an example of that.

"When a boxer is knocked down, the referee shall order the opponent to the farthest neutral corner of the ring and pick up the count from the timekeeper or the official counting for knockdowns. Should the opponent fail to stay in the corner as directed by the referee, the referee shall stop the count and redirect the boxer to go back to the corner and resume the count at the point that it was interrupted. In the event of any knockdown, the timekeepers count will cease when picked up by the referee whose count shall prevail and be relied on by the boxers."  This is from the WBF rules. Im surprised Joe doesn't actually understand what a 10 count in boxing actually is. It is not as simple as 10 seconds as soon as you're down and you're immediately out. It is supposed to be a 10 second period in which the referee is assessing the condition of the fighter. It does not have to be exactly 10 perfect seconds. If the downed fighter is responsive and answering questions then the count may be 1 or 2 seconds slower.
